12374-8{_}989{_}The expression of fear vanished, and listlessness gave place to a springing elasticity of motion.
12374-8{_}990{_}Mr. Noble could ill afford to spare so large a sum for the luxury of benevolence, and he was well aware that the office of protector, which he had taken upon himself, must necessarily prove expensive.
12374-8{_}991{_}But when he witnessed her radiant happiness, he could not regret that he had obeyed the generous impulse of his heart.
12374-8{_}992{_}Now, for the first time, she was completely identified with the vision of that fairy child who had so captivated his fancy four years before.
12374-8{_}993{_}He never forgot the tones of her voice, and the expression of her eyes, when she kissed his hand at parting, and said, "I thank you, Sir, for buying me."
12374-8{_}994{_}SCENE III.
12374-8{_}995{_}In a world like this, it is much easier to plan generous enterprises than to carry them into effect.
12374-8{_}996{_}After Mr. Noble had purchased the child, he knew not how to provide a suitable home for her.
12374-8{_}997{_}At first, he placed her with his colored washerwoman.
12374-8{_}998{_}But if she remained in that situation, though her bodily wants would be well cared for, she must necessarily lose much of the refinement infused into her being by that early environment of elegance, and that atmosphere of love.
12374-8{_}999{_}He did not enter into any analysis of his motives in wishing her to be so far educated as to be a pleasant companion for himself.
12374-8{_}1000{_}The only question he asked himself was, How he would like to have his sister treated, if she had been placed in such unhappy circumstances.
12374-8{_}1001{_}He knew very well what construction would be put upon his proceedings, in a society where handsome girls of such parentage were marketable; and he had so long tacitly acquiesced in the customs around him, that he might easily have viewed her in that light himself, had she not become invested with a tender and sacred interest from the circumstances in which he had first seen her, and the innocent, confiding manner in which she had implored him to supply the place of her father.
12374-8{_}1002{_}She was always presented to his imagination as Mr. Duncan's beloved daughter, never as Mr. Jackson's slave.
12374-8{_}1003{_}He said to himself, "May God bless me according to my dealings with this orphan!
12374-8{_}1004{_}May I never prosper, if I take advantage of her friendless situation!"
12374-8{_}1005{_}As for his _protégée_, she was too ignorant of the world to be disturbed by any such thoughts.
12374-8{_}1006{_}"May I call you Papa, as I used to call my father?"
12374-8{_}1007{_}said she.
12374-8{_}1008{_}For some reason, undefined to himself, the title was unpleasant to him.
12374-8{_}1009{_}It did not seem as if his sixteen years of seniority need place so wide a distance between them.
12374-8{_}1010{_}"No," he replied, "you shall be my sister."
12374-8{_}1011{_}And thenceforth she called him Brother Alfred, and he called her Loo Loo.
12374-8{_}1012{_}His curiosity was naturally excited to learn all he could of her history; and it was not long before he ascertained that her mother was a superbly handsome quadroon, from New Orleans, the daughter of a French merchant, who had given her many advantages of education, but from carelessness had left her to follow the condition of her mother, who was a slave.
12374-8{_}1013{_}Mr. Duncan fell in love with her, bought her, and remained strongly attached to her until the day of her death.
12374-8{_}1014{_}It had always been his intention to manumit her, but, from inveterate habits of procrastination, he deferred it, till the fatal fever attacked them both; and so _his_ child also was left to "follow the condition of her mother."
12374-8{_}1015{_}Having neglected to make a will, his property was divided among the sons of sisters married at a distance from him, and thus the little daughter, whom he had so fondly cherished, became the property of Mr. Jackson, who valued her as he would a handsome colt likely to bring a high price in the market.
12374-8{_}1016{_}She was too young to understand all the degradation to which she would be subjected, but she had once witnessed an auction of slaves, and the idea of being sold filled her with terror.
12374-8{_}1017{_}She had endured six months of corroding homesickness and constant fear, when Mr. Noble came to her rescue.
12374-8{_}1018{_}After a few weeks passed with the colored washerwoman, she was placed with an elderly French widow, who was glad to eke out her small income by taking motherly care of her, and giving her instruction in music and French.
12374-8{_}1019{_}The caste to which she belonged on the mother's side was rigorously excluded from schools, therefore it was not easy to obtain for her a good education in the English branches.
12374-8{_}1020{_}These Alfred took upon himself; and a large portion of his evenings was devoted to hearing her lessons in geography, arithmetic, and history.
12374-8{_}1021{_}Had any one told him, a year before, that hours thus spent would have proved otherwise than tedious, he would not have believed it.
12374-8{_}1022{_}But there was a romantic charm about this secret treasure, thus singularly placed at his disposal; and the love and gratitude he inspired gradually became a necessity of his life.
12374-8{_}1023{_}Sometimes he felt sad to think that the time must come when she would cease to be a child, and when the quiet, simple relation now existing between them must necessarily change.
12374-8{_}1024{_}He said to the old French lady, "By and by, when I can afford it, I will send her to one of the best schools at the North.
12374-8{_}1025{_}There she can become a teacher and take care of herself."
12374-8{_}1026{_}Madame Labassé smiled, shrugged her shoulders, and said, "_Nous verrons_."
12374-8{_}1027{_}She did not believe it.
12374-8{_}1028{_}The years glided on, and all went prosperously with the young merchant.
12374-8{_}1029{_}Through various conflicts with himself, his honorable resolution remained unbroken.
12374-8{_}1030{_}Loo Loo was still his sister.
12374-8{_}1031{_}She had become completely entwined with his existence.
12374-8{_}1032{_}Life would have been very dull without her affectionate greetings, her pleasant little songs, and the graceful dances she had learned to perform so well.
12374-8{_}1033{_}Sometimes, when he had passed a peculiarly happy evening in this fashion, Madame Labassé would look mischievous, and say, "But when do you think you shall send her to that school?"
12374-8{_}1034{_}True, she did not often repeat this experiment; for whenever she did it, the light went out of his countenance, as if an extinguisher were placed upon his soul.
12374-8{_}1035{_}"I _ought_ to do it," he said within himself; "but how _can_ I live without her?"
12374-8{_}1036{_}The French widow was the only person aware how romantic and how serious was this long episode in his life.
12374-8{_}1037{_}Some gentlemen, whom he frequently met in business relations, knew that he had purchased a young slave, whom he had placed with a French woman to be educated; but had he told them the true state of the case, they would have smiled incredulously.
12374-8{_}1038{_}Occasionally, they uttered some joke about the fascination which made him so indifferent to cards and horses; but the reserve with which he received such jests checked conversation on the subject, and all, except Mr. Grossman, discontinued such attacks, after one or two experiments.
12374-8{_}1039{_}As Mr. Noble's wealth increased, the wish grew stronger to place Louisa in the midst of as much elegance as had surrounded her in childhood.
12374-8{_}1040{_}When the house at Pine Grove was unoccupied, they often went out there, and it was his delight to see her stand under the Gothic arch of trees, a beautiful _tableau vivant_, framed in vines.
12374-8{_}1041{_}It was a place so full of heart-memories to her, that she always lingered there as long as possible, and never left it without a sigh.
12374-8{_}1042{_}In one place was a tree her father had planted, in another a rose or a jessamine her mother had trained.
12374-8{_}1043{_}But dearest of all was a recess among the pine-trees, on the side of a hill.
12374-8{_}1044{_}There was a rustic garden-chair, where her father had often sat with her upon his knee, reading wonderful story-books, bought for her on his summer excursions to New York or Boston.
12374-8{_}1045{_}In one of her visits with Alfred, she sat there and read aloud from "Lalla Rookh."
12374-8{_}1046{_}It was a mild winter day.
12374-8{_}1047{_}The sunlight came mellowed through the evergreens, a soft carpet of scarlet foliage was thickly strewn beneath their feet, and the air was redolent of the balmy breath of pines.
12374-8{_}1048{_}Fresh and happy in the glow of her fifteen summers, how could she otherwise than enjoy the poem?
12374-8{_}1049{_}It was like sparkling wine in a jewelled goblet.
12374-8{_}1050{_}Never before had she read anything aloud in tones so musically modulated, so full of feeling.
12374-8{_}1051{_}And the listener?
12374-8{_}1052{_}How worked the wine in _him?_ A voice within said, "Remember your vow, Alfred!
12374-8{_}1053{_}this charming Loo Loo is your adopted sister"; and he tried to listen to the warning.
12374-8{_}1054{_}She did not notice his tremor, when he rose hastily and said, "The sun is nearly setting.
12374-8{_}1055{_}It is time for my sister to go home."
12374-8{_}1056{_}"Home?"
12374-8{_}1057{_}she repeated, with a sigh.
12374-8{_}1058{_}"_This_ is my home.
12374-8{_}1059{_}I wish I could stay here always.
12374-8{_}1060{_}I feel as if the spirits of my father and mother were with us here."
12374-8{_}1061{_}Had she sighed for an ivory palace inlaid with gold, he would have wished to give it to her,--he was so much in love!
12374-8{_}1062{_}A few months afterward, Pine Grove was offered for sale.
12374-8{_}1063{_}He resolved to purchase it, and give her a pleasant surprise by restoring her to her old home, on her sixteenth birth-day.
12374-8{_}1064{_}Madame Labassé, who greatly delighted in managing mysteries, zealously aided in the preparations.
12374-8{_}1065{_}When the day arrived, Alfred proposed a long ride with Loo Loo,--in honor of the anniversary; and during their absence, Madame, accompanied by two household servants, established herself at Pine Grove.
12374-8{_}1066{_}When Alfred returned from the drive, he proposed to stop and look at the dear old place, to which his companion joyfully assented.
12374-8{_}1067{_}But nothing could exceed her astonishment at finding Madame Labassé there, ready to preside at a table spread with fruit and flowers.
12374-8{_}1068{_}Her feelings overpowered her for a moment, when Alfred said, "Dear sister, you said you wished you could live here always; and this shall henceforth be your home."
12374-8{_}1069{_}"You are too good!"
12374-8{_}1070{_}she exclaimed, and was about to burst into tears.
12374-8{_}1071{_}But he arrested their course by saying, playfully, "Come, Loo Loo, kiss my hand, and say, 'Thank you, Sir, for buying me.'
12374-8{_}1072{_}Say it just as you did six years ago, you little witch!"
12374-8{_}1073{_}Her swimming eyes smiled like sunshine through an April shower, and she went through the pantomime, which she had often before performed at his bidding.
12374-8{_}1074{_}Madame stepped in with her little jest: "But, Sir, when do you think you shall send her to that _pension_?"
12374-8{_}1075{_}"Never mind," he replied, abruptly; "Let us be happy!"
12374-8{_}1076{_}And he moved toward the table to distribute the fruit.
12374-8{_}1077{_}It was an inspiring spring-day, and ended in the loveliest of evenings.
12374-8{_}1078{_}The air was filled with the sweet breath of jessamines and orange-blossoms.
12374-8{_}1079{_}Madame touched the piano, and, in quick obedience to the circling sound, Alfred and Loo Loo began to waltz.
12374-8{_}1080{_}It was long before youth and happiness grew weary of the revolving maze.
12374-8{_}1081{_}But when at last she complained of dizziness, he playfully whirled her out upon the piazza, and placed her on a lounge under the Cherokee rose her mother had trained, which was now a mass of blossoms.
12374-8{_}1082{_}He seated himself in front of her, and they remained silent for some minutes, watching the vine-shadows play in the moonlight.
12374-8{_}1083{_}As Loo Loo leaned on the balustrade, the clustering roses hung over her in festoons, and trailed on her white muslin drapery.
12374-8{_}1084{_}Alfred was struck, as he had been many times before, with the unconscious grace of her attitude.
12374-8{_}1085{_}In imagination, he recalled his first vision of her in early childhood, the singular circumstance that had united their destinies, and the thousand endearing experiences which day by day had strengthened the tie.
12374-8{_}1086{_}As these thoughts passed through his mind, he gazed upon her with devouring earnestness.
12374-8{_}1087{_}She was too beautiful, there in the moonlight, crowned with roses!
12374-8{_}1088{_}"Loo Loo, do you love me?"
12374-8{_}1089{_}he exclaimed.
12374-8{_}1090{_}The vehemence of his tone startled her, as she sat there in a mood still and dreamy as the landscape.
12374-8{_}1091{_}She sprang up, and, putting her arm about his neck, answered, "Why, Alfred, you _know_ your sister loves you."
12374-8{_}1092{_}"Not as a brother, not as a brother, dear Loo Loo," he said, impatiently, as he drew her closely to his breast.
12374-8{_}1093{_}"Will you be my love?
12374-8{_}1094{_}Will you be my wife?"
12374-8{_}1095{_}In the simplicity of her inexperience, and the confidence induced by long habits of familiar reliance upon him, she replied, "I will be anything you wish."
12374-8{_}1096{_}No flower was ever more unconscious of a lover's burning kisses than she was of the struggle in his breast.
12374-8{_}1097{_}His feelings had been purely compassionate in the beginning of their intercourse; his intentions had been purely kind afterward; but he had gone on blindly to the edge of a slippery precipice.
12374-8{_}1098{_}Human nature should avoid such dangerous passes.
12374-8{_}1099{_}Reviewing that intoxicating evening in a calmer mood, he was dissatisfied with his conduct.
12374-8{_}1100{_}In vain he said to himself that he had but followed a universal custom; that all his acquaintance would have laughed in his face, had he told them of the resolution so bravely kept during six years.
12374-8{_}1101{_}The remembrance of his mother's counsels came freshly to his mind; and the accusing voice of conscience said, "She was a friendless orphan, whom misfortune ought to have rendered sacred.
12374-8{_}1102{_}What to you is the sanction of custom?
12374-8{_}1103{_}Have you not a higher law within your own breast?"
12374-8{_}1104{_}He tried to silence the monitor by saying, "When I have made a little more money, I will return to the North.
12374-8{_}1105{_}I will marry Loo Loo on the way and she shall be acknowledged to the world as my wife, as she now is in my own soul."
12374-8{_}1106{_}Meanwhile, the orphan lived in her father's house as her mother had lived before her.
12374-8{_}1107{_}She never aided the voice of Alfred's conscience by pleading with him to make her his wife; for she was completely satisfied with her condition, and had undoubting faith that whatever he did was always the wisest and the best.
12374-8{_}1108{_}[To be continued.]
12374-8{_}1109{_}CHARLEY'S DEATH.
12374-8{_}1110{_}The wind got up moaning, and blew to a breeze;    I sat with my face closely pressed on the pane;    In a minute or two it began to rain,   And put out the sunset-fire in the trees.
12374-8{_}1111{_}In the clouds' black faces broke out dismay    That ran of a sudden up half the sky,    And the team, cutting ruts in the grass, went by,   Heavy and dripping with sweet wet hay.
12374-8{_}1112{_}Clutching the straws out and knitting his brow,    Walked Arthur beside it, unsteady of limb;    I stood up in wonder, for, following him,   Charley was used to be;--where was he now?
12374-8{_}1113{_}"'Tis like him," I said, "to be working thus late!"
12374-8{_}1114{_}--    I said it, but did not believe it was so;    He could not have staid in the meadow to mow,   With rain coming down at so dismal a rate.
12374-8{_}1115{_}"He's bringing the cows home."
12374-8{_}1116{_}--I choked at that lie:    They were huddled close by in a tumult and fret,    Some pawing the dry dust up out of the wet,   Some looking afield with their heads lifted high.
12374-8{_}1117{_}O'er the run, o'er the hilltop, and on through the gloom    My vision ran quick as the lightning could dart;    All at once the blood shocked and stood still in my heart;--   He was coming as never till then he had come!
12374-8{_}1118{_}Borne 'twixt our four work-hands, I saw through the fall    Of the rain, and the shadows so thick and so dim,    They had taken their coats off and spread them on him,   And that he was lying out straight,--that was all!
12374-8{_}1119{_}THE CATACOMBS OF ROME.
12374-8{_}1120{_}[Continued.]
12374-8{_}1121{_}Custodit Dominus emnia ossa eorum.
12374-8{_}1122{_}Ps.
12374-8{_}1123{_}xxxiii.
12374-8{_}1124{_}20   III.
12374-8{_}1125{_}Not quite two miles from the city-gate known as the Porta Pia, there stands, on the left hand of the Nomentan Way, the ancient, and, until lately, beautiful, Church of St. Agnes outside the Walls.
12374-8{_}1126{_}The chief entrance to it descends by a flight of wide steps; for its pavement is below the level of the ground, in order to afford easy access to the catacombs known as those of St. Agnes, which opened out from it and stretched away in interlacing passages under the neighboring fields.
12374-8{_}1127{_}It was a quiet, retired place, with the sacredness that invests every ancient sanctuary, in which the prayers and hymns of many generations have risen.
12374-8{_}1128{_}The city was not near enough to disturb the stillness within its walls; little vineyards, and plots of market-garden, divided from each other by hedges of reeds and brambly roses, with wider open fields in the distance, lay around it; a deserted convent stood at its side; its precious marble columns were dulled and the gold ground of its mosaics was dimmed by the dust of centuries; its pavement was deeply worn; and its whole aspect was that of seclusion and venerable age, without desertion and without decay.
12374-8{_}1129{_}The story of St. Agnes is one of those which at the beginning of the fourth century became popular among the Christians and in the Church of Rome.
12374-8{_}1130{_}The martyrdom, under most cruel tortures and terrors, of a young girl, who chose to die rather than yield her purity or her faith, and who died with entire serenity and peace, supported by divine consolations, caused her memory to be cherished with an affection and veneration similar to that in which the memory of St. Cecilia was already held,--and very soon after her death, which is said to have taken place in the year 304, she was honored as one of the holiest of the disciples of the Lord.
12374-8{_}1131{_}Her story has been a favorite one through all later ages; poetry and painting have illustrated it; and wherever the Roman faith has spread, Saint Agnes has been one of the most beloved saints both of the rich and the poor, of the great and of the humble.
12374-8{_}1132{_}In her Acts[A] it is related that she was buried by her parents in a meadow on the Nomentan Way.
12374-8{_}1133{_}Here, it is probable, a cemetery had already for some time existed; and it is most likely that the body of the Saint was laid in one of the common tombs of the catacombs.
12374-8{_}1134{_}The Acts go on to tell, that her father and mother constantly watched at night by her grave, and once, while watching, "they saw, in the mid silence of the night, an army of virgins, clothed in woven garments of gold, passing by with a great light.
12374-8{_}1135{_}And in the midst of them they beheld the most blessed virgin Agnes, shining in a like dress, and at her right hand a lamb whiter than snow.
12374-8{_}1136{_}At this sight, great amazement took possession of her parents and of those who were with them.
12374-8{_}1137{_}But the blessed Agnes asked the holy virgins to stay their advance for a moment, when she said to her parents, 'Behold, weep not for me as for one dead, but rejoice with me and wish me joy; for with all these I have received a shining seat, and I am united in heaven to Him whom while on earth I loved with all my heart.'
12374-8{_}1138{_}And with these words she passed on."
12374-8{_}1139{_}The report of this vision was spread among the Christians of Rome.
12374-8{_}1140{_}The pleasing story was received into willing hearts; and the memory of the virgin was so cherished, that her name was soon given to the cemetery where she had been buried, and, becoming a favorite resting-place of the dead, its streets were lengthened by the addition of many graves.
12374-8{_}1141{_}[Footnote A: This is the name given to the accounts of the saints and martyrs composed in early times for the use of the Church.]
12374-8{_}1142{_}Not many years afterwards, Constantia, the daughter of the Emperor Constantine, suffering from a long and painful disease, for which she found no relief, heard of the marvellous vision, and was told of many wonderful cures that had been wrought at the tomb and by the intercession of the youthful Saint.
12374-8{_}1143{_}She determined, although a pagan, to seek the aid of which such great things were told; and going to the grave of Agnes at night, she prayed for relief.
12374-8{_}1144{_}Falling suddenly into a sweet sleep, the Saint appeared to her, and promised her that she should be made well, if she would believe in the Lord Jesus Christ.
12374-8{_}1145{_}She awoke, as the story relates, full of faith, and found herself well.
12374-8{_}1146{_}Moved with gratitude, she besought her father to build a church on the spot in honor of Saint Agnes, and in compliance with her wish, and in accordance with his own disposition to erect suitable temples for the services of his new faith, Constantine built the church, which a few centuries later was rebuilt in its present form and adorned with the mosaics that still exist.
12374-8{_}1147{_}Nearly about the same time a circular building was erected hard by the church, designed as a mausoleum for Constantia and other members of the imperial family.
12374-8{_}1148{_}The Mausoleum of Hadrian was occupied by the bodies of heathen emperors and empresses, and filled with heathen associations.
12374-8{_}1149{_}New tombs were needed for the bodies of those who professed to have revolted from heathenism.
12374-8{_}1150{_}The marble pillars of the Mausoleum of Constantia were taken from more ancient and nobler buildings, its walls were lined with mosaics, and her body was laid in a splendid sarcophagus of porphyry.
12374-8{_}1151{_}In the thirteenth century, after Constantia had been received into the liberal community of Roman saints, her mausoleum was consecrated as a church and dedicated to her honor.
12374-8{_}1152{_}A narrow, unworn path leads to it from the Church of St. Agnes; it has been long left uncared-for and unfrequented, and, stripped of its movable ornaments, it is now in a half-ruinous condition.
12374-8{_}1153{_}But its decay is more impressive than the gaudy brightness of more admired and renovated buildings.
12374-8{_}1154{_}The weeds that grow in the crevices of its pavement and hang over the capitals of its ancient pillars, the green mould on its walls, the cracks in its mosaics, are better and fuller of suggestion to the imagination than the shiny surface and the elaborate finish of modern restorations.
12374-8{_}1155{_}Restoration in these days always implies irreverence and bad taste.
12374-8{_}1156{_}But the architecture of this old building and the purpose for which it was originally designed present a marked example of the rapidity of the change in the character of the Christians with the change of their condition at Rome, during the reign of Constantine.
12374-8{_}1157{_}The worldliness that follows close on prosperity undermined the spirit of faith; the pomp and luxury of the court and the palace were carried into the forms of worship, into the construction of churches, into the manner of burial.
12374-8{_}1158{_}Social distinctions overcame the brotherhood in Christ.
12374-8{_}1159{_}Riches paved an easy way into the next world, and power set up guards around it.
12374-8{_}1160{_}Imperial remains were not to mingle with common dust, and the mausoleum of the princess rose above the rock-hewn and narrow grave of the martyr and saint.
12374-8{_}1161{_}The present descent into the catacombs that lie near the churches of St. Agnes and St. Constantia is by an entrance in a neighboring field, made, after the time of persecution, to accommodate those who might desire to visit the underground chapels and holy graves.
12374-8{_}1162{_}A vast labyrinth of streets spreads in every direction from it.
12374-8{_}1163{_}Many chambers have been cut in the rock at the side of the passages,--some for family burial-places, some for chapels, some for places of instruction for those not yet fully entered into the knowledge of the faith.
12374-8{_}1164{_}It is one of the most populous of the subterranean cemeteries, and one of the most interesting, from the great variety in its examples of underground architectural construction, and from the number of the paintings that are found upon its walls.
12374-8{_}1165{_}But its peculiar interest is, that it affords at one point a marked example of the connection of an _arenarium_, or pit from which _pozzolana_ was extracted, with the streets of the cemetery itself.
12374-8{_}1166{_}At this point, the bed of compact _tufa_, in which the graves are dug, degenerates into friable and loosely compacted volcanic sand,--and it was here, very probably, that the cemetery was begun, at a time when every precaution had to be used by the Christians to prevent the discovery of their burial-places.
12374-8{_}1167{_}No other of the catacombs gives a clearer exhibition of the differences in construction resulting from the different objects of excavation.
12374-8{_}1168{_}In the Acts known as those of St. Valentine it is related, that in the time of Claudius many Christians were condemned to work in certain sand-pits.
12374-8{_}1169{_}Under cover of such opportunities, occasions might be found in which hidden graves could be formed in the neighboring harder soil.
12374-8{_}1170{_}In digging out the sand, the object was to take out the greatest quantity consistent with safety, leaving only such supports as were necessary to hold up the superincumbent earth.
12374-8{_}1171{_}There are few regular paths, but wide spaces with occasional piers,--the passages being of sufficient width to admit of the entrance of beasts of burden, and even of carts.
12374-8{_}1172{_}The soil crumbles so easily, that no row of excavations one above another could be made in it; for the stroke of the pick-axe brings it down in loose masses.
12374-8{_}1173{_}The whole aspect of the sand-pit contrasts strikingly with that of the catacombs, with their three-feet wide galleries, their perpendicular walls, and their tier on tier of graves.
12374-8{_}1174{_}The stratum of pozzolana at the Catacombs of St. Agnes overlies a portion of the more solid stratum of tufa, and the entrance to the sand-pit from the cemetery is by steps leading up from the end of a long gallery.
12374-8{_}1175{_}Such an entrance could have been easily concealed; and the tufa cut out for the graves, after having been reduced to the condition of pozzolana, might easily at night have been brought up to the floor of the pit.
12374-8{_}1176{_}In many of the Acts of the Martyrs it is said that they were buried _in Arenario_, "in the sand-pit,"--an expression which, there seems no good reason for doubting, meant in the catacombs whose entrance was at the sand-pit, they not having yet received a distinctive name.
12374-8{_}1177{_}It is difficult to convey to a distant reader even a small share of the interest with which one sees on the spot evidences of the reality of the precautions with which, in those early centuries, the Christians of Rome were forced to guard themselves against a persecution which extended to their very burial-places,--or even of the interest with which one walks through the unchanged paths dug out of the rock by this _tenebrosa et lucifugax natio_.
12374-8{_}1178{_}In the midst of the obscurity of history and the fog of fable, here is the solid earth giving evidence of truth.
12374-8{_}1179{_}Here one sees where, by the light of his dim candle, the solitary digger hollowed out the grave of one of the near followers of the apostles; and here one reads in hasty and ill-spelt inscriptions something of the affection and of the faith of those who buried their dead in the sepulchre dug in the rock.
12374-8{_}1180{_}The Christian Rome underground is a rebuke to the Papal Rome above it; and, from the worldly pomp, the tedious forms, the trickeries, the mistakes, the false claims and falser assertions, the empty architecture that reveals the infidelity of its builders, the gross materialism, and the crass superstition of the Roman Church, one turns with relief of heart and eyes to the poverty and bareness of the dark and narrow catacombs, and to the simple piety of the words found upon their graves.
12374-8{_}1181{_}In them is at once the exhibition and the promise of a purer Christianity.
12374-8{_}1182{_}In them, indeed, one may see only too plainly the evidences of ignorance, the beginnings of superstitions, the first, traces of the corruption of the truth, the proofs of false zeal and of foolish martyrdoms,--but with these are also to be plainly seen the purity and the spirituality of elevated Christian faith.
12374-8{_}1183{_}In the service of the Roman Church used at the removal of the bodies of the holy martyrs from their graves in the catacombs is a prayer in which are the words,--"Thou hast set the bodies of thy soldiers as guards around the walls of this thy beloved Jerusalem";--and as one passes from catacomb to catacomb, it is, indeed, as if he passed from station to station of the encircling camp of the great army of the martyrs.
12374-8{_}1184{_}Leaving the burial-place of St. Agnes, we continue along the Nomentan Way to the seventh milestone from Rome.
12374-8{_}1185{_}Here the Campagna stretches on either side in broad, unsheltered sweeps.
12374-8{_}1186{_}Now and then a rough wall crosses the fields, marking the boundaries of one of the great farms into which the land is divided.
12374-8{_}1187{_}On the left stands a low farm-house, with its outlying buildings, and at a distance on each side the eye falls on low square brick towers of the Middle Ages, and on the ruinous heaps of more ancient tombs.
12374-8{_}1188{_}The Sabine mountains push their feet far down upon the plain, covered with a gray-green garment of olive-woods.
12374-8{_}1189{_}Few scenes in the Campagna are more striking, from the mingling of barrenness and beauty, from the absence of imposing monumental ruins and the presence of old associations.
12374-8{_}1190{_}The turf of the wide fields was cropped in the winter by the herds driven down at that season from the recesses of the Neapolitan mountains, and the irregular surface of the soil afforded no special indications of treasures buried beneath it.
12374-8{_}1191{_}But the Campagna is full of hidden graves and secreted buildings.
12374-8{_}1192{_}In the Acts of the Martyrdom of St. Alexander, who, according to the story of the Church, was the sixth successor of St. Peter, and who was put to death in the persecution of Trajan, in the year 117, it was said that his body was buried by a Roman lady, Severina, "on her farm, at the seventh milestone from Rome on the Nomentan Way."
12374-8{_}1193{_}These Acts, however, were regarded as apocryphal, and their statement had drawn but little attention to the locality.
12374-8{_}1194{_}In the spring of 1855, a Roman archaeologist, Signore Guidi, obtained permission from the Propaganda, by whom the land was now held, as a legacy from the last of the Stuarts, the Cardinal York, to make excavations upon it.
12374-8{_}1195{_}Beginning at a short distance from the road, on the right hand, and proceeding carefully, he soon struck upon a flight of steps formed of pieces of broken marble, which, at about fifteen feet below the surface of the ground, ended upon a floor paved with bits of marble, tombstones, and mosaics.
12374-8{_}1196{_}As the work proceeded, it disclosed the walls of an irregular church, that had been constructed, like that of St. Agnes, partially beneath the soil, for the purpose of affording an entrance into adjoining catacombs.
12374-8{_}1197{_}Remains of the altar were found, and portions of the open-work marble screen which had stood before it over the crypt in which the bodies of St. Alexander and one of his fellow-martyrs had been placed.
12374-8{_}1198{_}A part of the inscription on its border was preserved, and read as follows: ET ALEXANDRO DEDICATUS VOTUM POSUIT CONSECRANTE URSO EPISCOPO,--"Dedicatus placed this in fulfilment of a vow to ---- and Alexander, the Bishop Ursus consecrating it."
12374-8{_}1199{_}The Acts supply the missing name of Eventius,--an aged priest, who, it was said, had conversed with some of the apostles themselves.
12374-8{_}1200{_}His greater age had at that early and simple time given him the place of honor in the inscription and in men's memory before the youthful, so-called, Pope Alexander.
12374-8{_}1201{_}Probably this little church had been built in the fourth century, and here a bishop had been appointed to perform the rites within it.
12374-8{_}1202{_}It was a strange and touching discovery, that of this long-buried, rude country-church,--the very existence of which had been forgotten for more than a thousand years.
12374-8{_}1203{_}On the 3d of May, 1855, the day set apart in the calendar to the honor of the saints to whom it was consecrated, the holy services were once more performed upon the ancient altar of the roofless sanctuary.
12374-8{_}1204{_}The voices of priest and choir sounded through the long silent chapels, while the larks sang their hymns of gladness over the fields above.
12374-8{_}1205{_}On the rough floor, inscriptions, upon which, in the early centuries, the faithful had knelt, were again read by kneeling worshippers.
12374-8{_}1206{_}On one broken slab of marble was the word MARTYR; on another, the two words, SPARAGINA FIDELIS; on another, POST VARIAS CURAS, POST LONGE MONITA VITAE.
12374-8{_}1207{_}The catacombs opening from the church have not been entered to a great distance, and though more rudely excavated than most of those nearer the city, as if intended for the burial-places of a poorer population, they are of peculiar interest because many of their graves remain in their original state, and here and there, in the mortar that fastens their tiled fronts, portions of the vessel of glass or pottery that held the collected blood of the martyr laid within are still undisturbed.
12374-8{_}1208{_}No pictures of any size or beauty adorn the uneven walls, and no chapels are hollowed out within them.
12374-8{_}1209{_}Most of the few inscriptions are scratched upon the mortar,--_Spiritus tuus in bono quiescat_,--but now and then a bit of marble, once used for a heathen inscription, bears on its other side some Christian words.
12374-8{_}1210{_}None of the inscriptions within the church which bear a date are later than the end of the fifth century, and it seems likely that shortly after this time this church of the Campagna was deserted, and its roof falling in, it was soon concealed under a mass of rubbish and of earth, and the grass closed it with its soft and growing protection.
12374-8{_}1211{_}During two years, the uncovered church, with its broken pillars, its cracked altar, its imperfect mosaics, its worn pavement, remained open to the sky, in the midst of solitude.
12374-8{_}1212{_}But how could anything with such simple and solemn associations long escape desecration at Rome?
12374-8{_}1213{_}How could such an opportunity for _restoration_ be passed over?
12374-8{_}1214{_}How could so sacred and venerable a locality be protected from modern superstition and ecclesiastical zeal?
12374-8{_}1215{_}In the spring of 1837, preparations were being made for building upon the ground, and a Carthusian convent, it was said, was to be erected, which would enclose within its lifeless walls the remains of the ancient church.
12374-8{_}1216{_}Once more, then, it is to be shut out of the sky; and now it is not Nature that asserts her predominance, protecting while she conceals, and throwing her mantle over the martyrs' graves to keep them from sacrilege,--but she is driven away by the builders of the papal court, and all precious old associations are incongruous with those of modern Roman architecture and Roman conventual discipline.
12374-8{_}1217{_}One morning, in the spring of 1855, shortly after the discovery had been made, the Pope went out to visit the Church of St. Alexander.
12374-8{_}1218{_}On his return, he stopped to rest in the unoccupied convent adjoining the Church of St. Agnes.
12374-8{_}1219{_}Here there was a considerable assemblage of those who had accompanied him, and others who were admitted at this place to join his suite.
12374-8{_}1220{_}They were in the second story of the building, and the Pope was in the act of addressing them, when suddenly the old floor, unable to support the unaccustomed weight, gave way, and most of the company fell with it to the floor below.
12374-8{_}1221{_}The Pope was thrown down, but did not fall through.
12374-8{_}1222{_}The moment was one of great confusion and alarm, the etiquette of the court was disturbed, but no person was killed and no one dangerously hurt.
12374-8{_}1223{_}In common language and in Roman belief, it was a miraculous escape.
12374-8{_}1224{_}The Pope, attributing his safety to the protection of the Virgin and of St. Agnes, determined at once that the convent should be rebuilt and reoccupied, and the church restored.
12374-8{_}1225{_}The work is now complete, and all the ancient charm of time and use, all the venerable look of age and quiet, have been laboriously destroyed, and gaudy, inharmonious color, gilding and polish have been substituted in their place.
12374-8{_}1226{_}The debased taste and the unfeeling ignorance of restorers have been employed, as so often in Italy, to spoil and desecrate the memorials of the past; and the munificence of Pius, _Munificentia Pii IX._, is placarded on the inner walls.
12374-8{_}1227{_}One is too frequently reminded at Rome of the old and new lamps in the story of Aladdin.
12374-8{_}1228{_}We turn reluctantly from the Nomentan Way, and passing through Rome, we go out of the gate which opens on the Appian.
12374-8{_}1229{_}About a mile from the present wall, just where the road divides before coming to the Catacombs of St. Callixtus, a little, ugly, white church, of the deformed architecture of the seventeenth century, recalls, by its name of _Domine quo vadis?_ "O Lord, whither goest thou?"
12374-8{_}1230{_}one of the most impressive, one of the earliest and simplest, of the many legends of the legendary religious annals of Rome.
12374-8{_}1231{_}It relates, that, at the time of the persecution of Nero, St. Peter, being then in Rome, was persuaded to fly secretly from the city, in the hope of escaping from the near peril.
12374-8{_}1232{_}Just as he reached this place, trembling, we may well believe, not more with fear than with doubt, while past scenes rose vividly before him, and the last words heard from his Master's lips came with a flood of self-reproach into his heart,--as he hurried silently along, with head bowed down, in the gray twilight, he became suddenly aware of a presence before him, and, looking up, beheld the form of that beloved Master whom he was now a second time denying.
12374-8{_}1233{_}He beheld him, moreover, in the act of bearing his cross.
12374-8{_}1234{_}Peter, with his old ardor, did not wait to be addressed, but said, _Domine, quo vadis?_--"O Lord, whither goest thou?"
12374-8{_}1235{_}The Saviour, looking at him as he had looked but once before, replied, _Venio Romam iterum crucifigi_,--"I come to Rome to be crucified a second time"; and thereupon disappeared.
12374-8{_}1236{_}Peter turned, reëntered the gate, and shortly after was crucified for his Lord's sake.
12374-8{_}1237{_}His body, it is said, was laid away in a grave on the Vatican Hill, where his great church was afterwards built.
12374-8{_}1238{_}And here we come upon another legend, which takes us out again on the Appian Way, to the place where now stands the Church of St. Sebastian.
12374-8{_}1239{_}St. Gregory the Great relates in one of his letters, that, not long after St. Peter and St. Paul had suffered martyrdom, some Christians came from the East to Rome to find the bodies of these their countrymen, which they desired to carry back with them to their own land.
12374-8{_}1240{_}They so far succeeded as to gain possession of the bodies, and to carry them as far as the second milestone on the Appian Way.
12374-8{_}1241{_}Here they paused, and when they attempted to carry the bodies farther, so great a storm of thunder and lightning arose, that they were terrified, and did not venture to repeat their attempt.
12374-8{_}1242{_}By this time, also, the Romans had become aware of the carrying off of the sacred bodies, and, coming out from the city, recovered possession of them.
12374-8{_}1243{_}One of the old pictures on the wall of the portico of the ancient basilica of St. Peter's preserved a somewhat different version of the legend, representing the Romans as falling violently upon the Oriental robbers, and compelling them, with a storm of blows, to yield up the possession of the relics they were carrying away by stealth.
12374-8{_}1244{_}But the legend went on further to state, that, on the spot where they thus had regained the bodies of their saints, the Romans made a deep hole in the ground, and laid them away within it very secretly.
12374-8{_}1245{_}Here for some time they rested, but at length were restored to their original tombs, the one on the Ostian Way, the other on the Vatican.
12374-8{_}1246{_}But St. Peter was again to be laid in this secret chamber in the earth on the Appian Way.
12374-8{_}1247{_}In the episcopate of the saint and scoundrel Callixtus, the Emperor Elagabalus, with characteristic extravagance and caprice, resolved to make a circus on the Vatican, wide enough for courses of chariots drawn by four elephants abreast.
12374-8{_}1248{_}All the older buildings in the way were to be destroyed, to gratify this imperial whim; and Callixtus, fearing lest the Christian cemetery, and especially the tomb of the prince of the apostles might be discovered and profaned, removed the body of St. Peter once more to the Appian Way.
12374-8{_}1249{_}Here it lay for forty years, and round it and near it an underground cemetery was gradually formed; and it was to this burial-place, first of all, that the name Catacomb,[B] now used to denote all the underground cemeteries, was applied.
12374-8{_}1250{_}[Footnote B: A word, the derivation of which is not yet determined.
12374-8{_}1251{_}The first instance of its use is in the letter of Gregory from which we derive the legend.
12374-8{_}1252{_}This letter was written A.D.
12374-8{_}1253{_}594.]
12374-8{_}1254{_}Though at length St. Peter was restored to the Vatican, from which he has never since been removed, and where his grave is now hidden by his church, the place where he had lain so long was still esteemed sacred.
12374-8{_}1255{_}The story of St. Sebastian relates how, after his martyred body had been thrown into the Cloaca Maxima, that his friends might not have the last satisfaction of giving it burial, he appeared in a vision to Lucina, a Roman lady, told her where his body might be found, and bade her lay it in a grave near that in which the apostles had rested.
12374-8{_}1256{_}This was done, and less than a century afterward a church rose to mark the place of his burial, and connected with it, Pope Damasus, the first great restorer and adorner of the catacombs, [A.D. 266-285,] caused the chamber that was formed below the surface of the ground around the grave of the apostles to be lined with wide slabs of marble, and to be consecrated as a subterranean chapel.
12374-8{_}1257{_}It is curious enough that this pious work should have been performed, as is learned from an inscription set up here by Damasus himself, in fulfilment of a vow, on the extinction among the Roman clergy of the party of Ursicinus, his rival.
12374-8{_}1258{_}This custom of propitiating the favor of the saints by fair promises was thus early established.
12374-8{_}1259{_}It was soon found out that it was well to have a friend at court with whom a bargain could be struck.
12374-8{_}1260{_}If the adorning of this chapel was all that Damasus had to pay for the getting rid of his rival's party, the bargain was an easy one for him.
12374-8{_}1261{_}There had been terrible and bloody fights in the Roman streets between the parties of the contending aspirants for the papal seat.
12374-8{_}1262{_}Ursicinus had been driven from Rome, but Damasus had had trouble with the priests of his faction.
12374-8{_}1263{_}Some of them had been rescued, as he was hurrying them off to prison, and had taken refuge with their followers in the Basilica of St. Maria Maggiore.
12374-8{_}1264{_}Damasus, with a mob of charioteers, gladiators, and others of the scum of Rome, broke into the church, and slew a hundred and sixty men and women who had been shut up within it.
12374-8{_}1265{_}Ursicinus, however, returned to the city; there were fresh disturbances, and a new massacre, on this occasion, in the Church of St. Agnes; and years passed before Damasus was established as undisputed ruler of the Church.
12374-8{_}1266{_}It was then, in fulfilment of the vow he had made during his troubles, that _Saint_ Damasus (for he became a saint long since, success being a great sanctifier) adorned the underground chapel of the apostles.
12374-8{_}1267{_}The entrance to it is through the modern basilica of St. Sebastian.
12374-8{_}1268{_}It is a low, semicircular chamber, with irregular walls, in which a row of arched graves (_arcosolia_) has been formed, which once were occupied, probably, by bodies of saints or martyrs.
12374-8{_}1269{_}Near the middle of the chapel is the well, about seven feet square, within which are the two graves, lined with marble, where the bodies of the apostles are said to have lain hid.
12374-8{_}1270{_}Fragments of painting still remain on the walls of this pit, and three faint and shadowy figures may be traced, which seem to represent the Saviour between St. Peter and St. Paul.
12374-8{_}1271{_}Over the mouth of the well stands an ancient altar.
12374-8{_}1272{_}However little credence may be given to the old legends concerning the place, it is impossible not to look with interest upon it.
12374-8{_}1273{_}For fifteen hundred years worshippers have knelt there as upon ground made holy by the presence of the two apostles.
12374-8{_}1274{_}The memory of their lives and of their teachings has, indeed, consecrated the place; and though superstition has often turned the light of that memory into darkness, yet here, too, has faith been strengthened, and courage become steadfast, and penitence been confirmed into holiness, by the remembrance of the zeal, the denial of Peter, and the forgiveness of his Master, by the remembrance of the conversion, the long service, the exhortations, and the death of Paul.
12374-8{_}1275{_}The catacombs proper, to which entrance may be had from the Basilica of St. Sebastian, are of little importance in themselves, and have lost, by frequent alteration and by the erection of works of masonry for their support, much that was characteristic of their original construction.
12374-8{_}1276{_}During a long period, while most of the other subterranean cemeteries were abandoned, this remained open, and was visited by numerous pilgrims.
12374-8{_}1277{_}It led visitors to the church, and the guardians of the church found it for their interest to keep it in good repair.
12374-8{_}1278{_}Thus, though its value as one of the early burial-places of the Christians was diminished, another interest attached to it through the character of some of those visitors who were accustomed to frequent its dark paths.
12374-8{_}1279{_}Saint Bridget found some of that wild mixture of materialism and mysticism, (a not uncommon mingling,) which passes under the name of her Revelations, in the solitude of these streets of the dead.
12374-8{_}1280{_}Here St. Philip Neri, the Apostle of Rome, the wise and liberal founder of the Oratorians, the still beloved saint of the Romans, was accustomed to spend whole nights in prayer and meditation.
12374-8{_}1281{_}Demons, say his biographers, and evil spirits assailed him on his way, trying to terrify him and turn him back; but he overcame them all.
12374-8{_}1282{_}Year after year he kept up this practice, and gained strength, in the solitude and darkness, and in the presence of the dead, to resist fiercer demons than any that had power to attack him from without.
12374-8{_}1283{_}And it is related, that, when St. Charles Borromeo, his friend, the narrow, but pure-minded reformer of the Church, came to Rome, from time to time, he, too, used to go at night to this cemetery, and watch through the long hours in penitence and prayer.
12374-8{_}1284{_}Such associations as these give interest to the cemetery of St. Sebastian's Church.
12374-8{_}1285{_}The preëminence which the Appian Way, _regina viarum_, held among the great streets leading from Rome,--not only as the road to the South and to the fairest provinces, but also because it was bordered along its course by the monumental tombs of the greatest Roman families,--was retained by it, as we have seen, as the street on which lay the chief Christian cemeteries.
12374-8{_}1286{_}The tombs of the Horatii, the Metelli, the Scipios, were succeeded by the graves of a new, less famous, but not less noble race of heroes.
12374-8{_}1287{_}On the edge of the height that rises just beyond the Church of St. Sebastian stand the familiar and beautiful ruins of the tomb of Cecilia Metella.
12374-8{_}1288{_}Of her who was buried in this splendid mausoleum nothing is known but what the three lines of the inscription still remaining on it tell us,--  CAECILIAE Q. CRETICI F. METELLAE CRASSI.
12374-8{_}1289{_}She was the daughter of Quintus, surnamed the Cretan, and the wife of Crassus.
12374-8{_}1290{_}But her tomb overlooks the ground beneath which, in a narrow grave, was buried a more glorious Cecilia.
12374-8{_}1291{_}[C] The contrast between the ostentation and the pride of the tombs of the heathen Romans, and the poor graves, hollowed out in the rock, of the Christians, is full of impressive suggestions.
12374-8{_}1292{_}The very closeness of their neighborhood to each other brings out with vivid effect the broad gulf of separation that lay between them in association, in affection, and in hopes.
12374-8{_}1293{_}[Footnote C: Guéranger, _Histoire de St. Cécile_.
12374-8{_}1294{_}p.
12374-8{_}1295{_}45.]
12374-8{_}1296{_}Coming out from the dark passages of the Catacombs of St. Callixtus, in the clear twilight of a winter's evening, one sees rising against the red glow of the sky the broken masses of the ancient tombs.
12374-8{_}1297{_}One city of the dead lies beneath the feet, another stretches before the eyes far out of sight.
12374-8{_}1298{_}The crowded history of Rome is condensed into one mighty spectacle.
12374-8{_}1299{_}The ambitions, the hates, the valor, the passions, the religions, the life and death of a thousand years are there; and, in the dimness of the dusky evening, troops of the dead rise before the imagination and advance in slow procession by opposite ways along the silent road.
12374-8{_}1300{_}[To be continued]         *       *       *       *       *   THE PURE PEARL OF DIVER'S BAY.
12374-8{_}1301{_}[Concluded.]
12374-8{_}1302{_}V   Did she talk of flesh and blood, when she said that she would find him?--The summer passed away; and when autumn came, it could not be said that search for the bodies of these fishermen was quite abandoned.
12374-8{_}1303{_}But no fragment of boat, nor body of father or son, ever came, by rumor or otherwise, to the knowledge of the people of the Bay.
12374-8{_}1304{_}The voyage was long to Clarice.
12374-8{_}1305{_}Marvellous strength and acuteness of vision come to the eyes of those who watch.
12374-8{_}1306{_}Keen grow the ears that listen.
12374-8{_}1307{_}The soldier's wife in the land of Nena Sahib inspires despairing ranks: "Dinna ye hear the pibroch?
12374-8{_}1308{_}Hark!
12374-8{_}1309{_}'The Campbells are coming!'"
12374-8{_}1310{_}--and at length, when the hope she lighted has gone out in sullen darkness, and they bitterly resent the joy she gave them,--lo, the bagpipes, banners, regiment!
12374-8{_}1311{_}The pibroch sounds, "The Campbells are coming!"
12374-8{_}1312{_}The Highlanders are in sight!--But, oh, the voyage was long,--and Clarice could see no sail, could hear no oar!
12374-8{_}1313{_}Clarice ceased to say that she must find the voyagers.
12374-8{_}1314{_}She ceased to talk of them.
12374-8{_}1315{_}She lived in these days a life so silent, and, as it seemed, so remote from other lives, that it quite passed the understanding of those who witnessed it.
12374-8{_}1316{_}Tears seldom fell from her eyes, complaints never;--but her interest was aroused by no temporal matter; she seemed, in her thoughts and her desires, as far removed as a spirit from the influences of the external world.
12374-8{_}1317{_}This state of being no person who lives by bread alone could have understood, or endured patiently, in one with whom in the affairs of daily life he was associated.
12374-8{_}1318{_}The Revelator was an exile in Patmos.
12374-8{_}1319{_}Dame Briton was convinced that Clarice was losing her wits.
12374-8{_}1320{_}Bondo Emmins yielded to the force of some inexplicable law, and found her fairer day by day.
12374-8{_}1321{_}To his view, she was like a vision moving through a dream, rather than like any actual woman; and though the drift of the vision seemed not towards him, he was more anxious to compel it than to accomplish any other purpose ever entertained.
12374-8{_}1322{_}The actual nearness, the apparent unattainableness, of that he coveted, excited in him such desires of conquest and possession as he would seek to appease in one way alone.
12374-8{_}1323{_}To win her would have been to the mind of any other inhabitant of Diver's Bay a feat as impracticable as the capture of the noble ghost of Hamlet's father, as he stands exorcized by Mrs. Kemble.
12374-8{_}1324{_}And yet, while her sorrow made her the pity and the wonder of the people, it did not keep her sacred from the reach of gossip.
12374-8{_}1325{_}Observing the frequency with which Bondo Emmins visited Old Briton's cabin, it was profanely said by some that the pale girl would ere long avert her eyes from the dead and fix them on the living.
12374-8{_}1326{_}Emmins had frequent opportunities for making manifest his good-will towards the family of Briton.
12374-8{_}1327{_}The old man fell on the ice one day and broke his thigh, and was constrained to lie in bed for many a day, and to walk with the help of crutches when he rose again.
12374-8{_}1328{_}Then was the young man's time to serve him like a son.
12374-8{_}1329{_}He brought a surgeon from the Port,--and the inefficiency of the man was not his fault, surely.
12374-8{_}1330{_}Through tedious days and nights Emmins sat by the old man's bedside, soothing pain, enlivening weariness, endeavoring to banish the gloomy elements that combined to make the cabin the abode of darkness.
12374-8{_}1331{_}He would have his own way, and no one could prevent him.
12374-8{_}1332{_}When Old Briton's money failed, his supplies did not.
12374-8{_}1333{_}Even Clarice was compelled to accept his service thankfully, and to acknowledge that she knew not how they could have managed without him in this strait.
12374-8{_}1334{_}The accident, unfortunate as it might be deemed, nevertheless exercised a most favorable influence over the poor girl's life.
12374-8{_}1335{_}It brought her soul back to her body, and spoke to her of wants and their supply,--of debts, of creditors,--of fish, and sea-weed, and the market,--of bread, and doctor's bills,--of her poor old father, and of her mother.
12374-8{_}1336{_}She came back to earth.
12374-8{_}1337{_}Now, henceforth, the support of the household was with her.
12374-8{_}1338{_}Bondo Emmins might serve her father,--she had no desire to prevent what was so welcome to the wretched old man,--but for herself, her mother, the house, no favor from him!
12374-8{_}1339{_}And thus Clarice rose up to rival Bondo in her ready courage.
12374-8{_}1340{_}When her father, at last careful, at last anxious, thoughtful of the future, began to express his fear, he met the ready assurance of his daughter that she should be able to provide all they should ever want; let him not be troubled; when the spring came, she would show him.
12374-8{_}1341{_}The spring came, and Clarice set to work as never in her industrious life before.
12374-8{_}1342{_}Day after day she gathered sea-weed, dried it, and carried it to town.
12374-8{_}1343{_}She went out with her mother in the fishing-boat, and the two women were equal in strength and courage to almost any two men of the Bay.
12374-8{_}1344{_}She filled the empty fish-barrels,--and promised to double the usual number.
12374-8{_}1345{_}She dried wagon-loads of finny treasure, and she made good bargains with the traders.
12374-8{_}1346{_}No one was so active, no one bade fair to turn the summer to such profit as Clarice.
12374-8{_}1347{_}She had come back to flesh and blood.--John came back from Patmos.
12374-8{_}1348{_}Her face grew brown with tan; it was not lovely as a fair ghost's, any longer; it was ruddy,--and her limbs grew strong.
12374-8{_}1349{_}Bondo Emmins marked these symptoms, and took courage.
12374-8{_}1350{_}People generally said, "She is well over her grief, and has set her heart on getting rich.
12374-8{_}1351{_}There is that much of her mother in her."
12374-8{_}1352{_}Others considered that Emmins was in the secret, and at the bottom of her serenity and diligence.
12374-8{_}1353{_}Dame Briton and her spouse were not one whit wiser than their neighbors.
12374-8{_}1354{_}They could not see that any half-work was impossible with Clarice,--that, if she had resolved, for their sake, to live as people must, who have bodies to respect and God-originated wants to supply, she must live by a ceaseless activity.
12374-8{_}1355{_}Because she had ascended far beyond tears, lamentation, helplessness, they thought she had forgotten.
12374-8{_}1356{_}Yes, they came to this conclusion, though now and then, not often, generally on some pleasant Sunday, when all her work was done, Clarice would go down to the Point and take her Sabbath rest there.
12374-8{_}1357{_}No danger of disturbance there!--of all bleak and desert places known to the people of Diver's Bay, that point was bleakest and most deserted.
12374-8{_}1358{_}The place was hers, then.
12374-8{_}1359{_}In this solitude she could follow her thoughts, and be led by them down to the ocean, or away to heavenly depths.
12374-8{_}1360{_}It was good for her to go there in quietness,--to rest in recollection.
12374-8{_}1361{_}Strength comes ever to the strong.
12374-8{_}1362{_}This pure heart had nothing to fear of sorrow.
12374-8{_}1363{_}Sorrow can only give the best it has to such as she.
12374-8{_}1364{_}Grief may weaken the selfish and the weak; it may make children of the foolish and drivellers; by grief the inefficient may come to the fulness of their inefficiency;--but out of the bitter cup the strong take strength, though it may be with shuddering.
12374-8{_}1365{_}One Sunday morning Clarice lingered longer about the house than usual, and Emmins, who had resolved, that, if she went that day to the Point, he would follow her, found her with her father and mother, talking merely for their pleasure,--if the languid tones of her voice and the absent look of her eyes were to be trusted.
12374-8{_}1366{_}Emmins thought that this moment was favorable to him.
12374-8{_}1367{_}He was sure of Dame Briton and the old man, and he almost believed that he was sure of Clarice.
12374-8{_}1368{_}Finding her now with her father and mother at home on this bright Sunday morning, one glance at her face surprised him and, almost before he was aware, he had spoken what he had hitherto so patiently refrained from speaking.
12374-8{_}1369{_}But the answer of Clarice still more surprised him.
12374-8{_}1370{_}With her eyes gazing out on the sea, she stood, the image of silence, while Bondo warily set forth his hopes.
12374-8{_}1371{_}Old Briton and the dame looked on and deemed the symptoms favorable.
12374-8{_}1372{_}But Clarice said,--  "Heart and hand I gave to him.
12374-8{_}1373{_}I am the wife of Luke;--how can I marry another?"
12374-8{_}1374{_}Bondo seemed eager to answer that question, for he hastily waved his hand toward Dame Briton, who began to speak.
12374-8{_}1375{_}"Luke will never come back," said he, gently expostulating.
12374-8{_}1376{_}"But I shall go to him," was the quiet reply.
12374-8{_}1377{_}Then the old people, whose hearts were in the wooing, broke out together,--and by their voices, if one should argue with them, strife was not far off.
12374-8{_}1378{_}Clarice staid one moment, as if to take in the burden of each eager voice; then she shook her head:--  "I am married already," she said; "I gave him my heart and my hand.
12374-8{_}1379{_}You would not rob Luke Merlyn?"
12374-8{_}1380{_}When she had so spoken, calmly, firmly, as if it were impossible that she should be moved or agitated by such speech as this she had heard, Clarice walked away to the beach, unmoored her father's boat, and rowed out into the Bay.
12374-8{_}1381{_}Bondo Emmins stood with the old people and gazed after her.
12374-8{_}1382{_}"Odd fish!"
12374-8{_}1383{_}he muttered.
12374-8{_}1384{_}"Never mind," said Old Briton, hobbling up and down the sand; "it's the first time she's been spoke to.
12374-8{_}1385{_}She'll come round.
12374-8{_}1386{_}I know Clarice."
12374-8{_}1387{_}"You know Clarice?"
12374-8{_}1388{_}broke in Dame Briton.
12374-8{_}1389{_}"You don't know her!
12374-8{_}1390{_}She isn't Clarice,--she's somebody else.
12374-8{_}1391{_}Who, I don't know."
12374-8{_}1392{_}"Hush!"
12374-8{_}1393{_}said Bondo, who had no desire that the couple should fall into a quarrel.
12374-8{_}1394{_}"I know who she is.
12374-8{_}1395{_}Don't plague her.
12374-8{_}1396{_}It will all come out right yet.
12374-8{_}1397{_}I'll wait.
12374-8{_}1398{_}But don't say anything to her about it.
12374-8{_}1399{_}Let me speak when the time comes.--Where's my pipe, Dame Briton?"
12374-8{_}1400{_}Emmins spent a good part of the day with the old people, and did not allow the conversation once to turn upon himself and Clarice.
12374-8{_}1401{_}But he talked of the improvements he should like to make in the old cabin, and they discussed the market, and entertained each other with recollections of past times, and with strange stories made up of odd imaginations and still more uncouth facts.
12374-8{_}1402{_}Supernatural influences were dwelt upon, and many a belief in superstitions belonging to childhood was confessed in peaceful unconsciousness of the fact that it was Clarice who had turned all their thoughts to-day from the great prosaic highway where plain facts have their endless procession.
12374-8{_}1403{_}VI.
12374-8{_}1404{_}Clarice went out alone in her fishing-boat, as during all the past week she had purposed to do when this day came, if it should prove favorable.
12374-8{_}1405{_}She wished to approach the Point thus,--and her purpose in so doing was such as no mortal could have suspected.
12374-8{_}1406{_}And yet, as in the fulfilment of this purpose she went, hastened from her delaying by the address of Bondo Emmins, it seemed to her as if her secret must be read by the three upon the beach.
12374-8{_}1407{_}She wore upon her neck, as she had worn since the days of her betrothal to Luke, the cord to which the pearl ring was attached.
12374-8{_}1408{_}The ring had never been removed; but now, as Clarice came near to the Point, she laid the oars aside, and with trembling hands untied the black cord and disengaged the ring, and drew it on her finger, that trembled like a leaf.
12374-8{_}1409{_}She was doing now what Luke had bidden her do,--and for his sake.
12374-8{_}1410{_}Until now she had always looked upon it as a ring of betrothal; henceforth it was her wedding-ring,--the evidence of her true marriage with Luke Merlyn.
12374-8{_}1411{_}O unseen husband, didst thou see her as anew she gave herself to love, to constancy, to duty?
12374-8{_}1412{_}She was floating toward the Point, when she knelt in the fishing-boat and plunged the hand that wore the ring under the bright cold water.
12374-8{_}1413{_}How bright, how cold it was!
12374-8{_}1414{_}It chilled Clarice; she shuddered; was she the bride of Death?
12374-8{_}1415{_}But she did not rise from her knees, neither withdraw her hand, until her vow, the vow she was there to speak, was spoken.
12374-8{_}1416{_}There she knelt alone in the great universe, with God and Luke Merlyn.
12374-8{_}1417{_}When at last she stood upon the Point, she had strength to meet her destiny, and patience to wait while it was being developed.
12374-8{_}1418{_}She knew her marriage covenant was blest, and filial duty was divested of every thought or notion that could tempt or deceive her.
12374-8{_}1419{_}Treading thus fearlessly among the high places of imagination, no prescience of mortal trouble could lurk among the mysterious shadows.
12374-8{_}1420{_}By her faith in the eternity of love she was greatly more than conqueror.
12374-8{_}1421{_}The day passed, and night drew near.
12374-8{_}1422{_}It was the purpose of Clarice to row home with the tide.
12374-8{_}1423{_}But a strange thing happened to her ere she set out to return.
12374-8{_}1424{_}As she stood looking out upon the sea, watching the waves as they rolled and broke upon the beach, a new token came to her from the deep.
12374-8{_}1425{_}Almost as she might have waited for Luke, she stood watching the onward drift; calculating the spot at which the waves would deposit their burden, she stood there when the plank was borne inland, to save it, if possible, from being dashed with violence on the rocks.
12374-8{_}1426{_}To this plank a child was bound,--a little creature that might be three years old.
12374-8{_}1427{_}At the sight of this form, and this helplessness, the heart of the woman seemed to break into sudden living flame.
12374-8{_}1428{_}She carried the plank down to a level spot with an energy that would have made light of a burden even ten times as great; she stooped upon the sand; she unbound the body; and she thought, "The child is dead!"
12374-8{_}1429{_}Nevertheless she took him in her arms; she dried his limbs with her apron; she wiped his face, and rubbed his hair;--but he gave no sign of life.
12374-8{_}1430{_}Then she wrapped him in her shawl, and laid him in the boat, and rowed home.
12374-8{_}1431{_}There was no one in the cabin when Clarice went in.
12374-8{_}1432{_}When Dame Briton came home, she found her daughter with a ring upon her finger, bending over the body of a child that lay upon her bed.
12374-8{_}1433{_}The dame was quickly brought into service, and there was no reason to fear that she would desist from her labors until she had received some evidence of death or life.
12374-8{_}1434{_}She and Clarice worked all night over the body of the child, and towards morning were rewarded by the result.
12374-8{_}1435{_}The boy's eyes opened, and he tried to speak.
12374-8{_}1436{_}By noon of that day he was lying in the arms of Clarice, deathly pallor on his little face; but he could speak, and his pretty eyes were open.
12374-8{_}1437{_}All those hours of mutual sympathy and striving, Dame Briton had been thinking to say, "Clarice, what's the ring for?"
12374-8{_}1438{_}But she had not said it, when, in the afternoon, Bondo Emmins came into the cabin, and saw Clarice with a beautiful boy in her arms, wrapped in her shawl, while before the fire some rags of infant garments were drying.
12374-8{_}1439{_}They talked over the boy's fortune and the night's work, the dame taking the chief conduct of the story; and Bondo was so much interested, and praised the child so much, and spoke with so much concern of the solitary, awful voyage the little one must have made, that, when he subsequently offered to take the child in his arms, Clarice let him go, and explained, when the young man began to talk to the boy, that he could not understand a word, neither could she make out the meaning of his speech.
12374-8{_}1440{_}Emmins heard Clarice say that she must go to the Port the next day and learn what vessel had been lost, and if any passengers were saved; and by daybreak he set out on that errand.
12374-8{_}1441{_}He returned early in the morning with the news that a merchantman, the "Gabriel," had gone down, and that cargo and crew were lost.
12374-8{_}1442{_}While he was telling this to Clarice he observed the ring upon her finger, and he coupled the appearing of that token with the serenity of the girl's face, and hailed his conclusion as one who hoped everything from change and nothing from constancy.
12374-8{_}1443{_}Clarice had found the boy in the place where she had looked for Luke that night when his cap was washed to her feet.
12374-8{_}1444{_}Over and over again she had said this to her father and mother while they busied themselves about the unconscious child; now she said it again to Bondo Emmins, as if there were some special significance in the fact, as indeed to her there was.
12374-8{_}1445{_}He was her child, and he should be her care, and she would call him Gabriel.
12374-8{_}1446{_}People could understand the burden imposed upon the laborious life of Clarice by this new, strange care.
12374-8{_}1447{_}But they did not see the exceeding great reward, nor how the love that lingered about a mere memory seemed blessed to the poor girl with a blessing of divine significance.
12374-8{_}1448{_}To make the child her own by some special act that should establish her right became the wish of Clarice.
12374-8{_}1449{_}It was not enough for her that she should toil for him while others slept, that she should stint herself in order to clothe him in a becoming manner, that she should suffer anxiety for him in the manifold forms best known to those who have endured it.
12374-8{_}1450{_}She had given herself to Luke, so that she feared no more from any man's solicitation.
12374-8{_}1451{_}She would fain assert her claim to this young life which Providence had given her.
12374-8{_}1452{_}But this desire was suggested by external influence, as her marriage covenant had been.
12374-8{_}1453{_}Now and then a missionary came down to Diver's Bay, and preached in the open air, or, if the weather disappointed him, in the great shed built for the protection of fish-barrels and for the drying of fish.
12374-8{_}1454{_}No surprising results had ever attended his preaching; the meetings were never large, though sometimes tolerably well attended; the preacher was almost a stranger to the people; and the wonder would have been a notable one, had there been any harvest to speak of in return for the seed he scattered.
12374-8{_}1455{_}The seed was good; but the fowls of the air were free to carry it away; the thorns might choke it, if they would; it was not protected from any wind that blew.
12374-8{_}1456{_}A few Sundays after Gabriel became the charge of Clarice, the missionary came and preached to the people about Baptism.
12374-8{_}1457{_}Though burdened with a multitude of cares which he had no right to assume, which kept him busy day and night in efforts lacking only the concentration that would have made them effective, the man was earnest in his labor and his speech, and it chanced now and then that a soul was ready for the truth he brought.
12374-8{_}1458{_}On this occasion he addressed the parents in their own behalf and that of their children.
12374-8{_}1459{_}The bright day, the magnificent view his eyes commanded from the place where he stood to address the handful of people, the truth, with whose importance he was impressed, made him eloquent.
12374-8{_}1460{_}He spoke with power, and Clarice Briton, holding the hand of little Gabriel, listened as she had never listened before.
12374-8{_}1461{_}"Death unto sin," this baptism signified, he said.
12374-8{_}1462{_}She looked at the child's bright face; she recalled the experience through which she had passed, by which she was able to comprehend these words.
12374-8{_}1463{_}She had passed through death; she had risen to life; for Luke was dead, and was alive again,--therefore she lived also.
12374-8{_}1464{_}Tears came into the girl's eyes, unexpected, abundant, as she listened to the missionary's pleading with these parents, to give their little ones to their Heavenly Father, and themselves to lives of holiness.
12374-8{_}1465{_}He would set the mark of the cross on their foreheads, he said, to show that they were Christ's servants;--and then he preached of Christ, seeking to soften the tough souls about him with the story of a divine childhood; and he verily talked to them as one should do who felt that in all his speaking their human hearts anticipated him.
12374-8{_}1466{_}It was not within the compass of his voice to reach that savage note which in brutal ignorance condemns, where loving justice never could condemn.
12374-8{_}1467{_}He had an apprehension of the vital truth that belief in the world's Saviour was not belief in a name, but the reception of that which Jesus embodied.
12374-8{_}1468{_}He came down to Diver's Bay, expecting to find human nature there, and the only pity was that he had not time to perform what he attempted.
12374-8{_}1469{_}Let us, however, thank him for his honest endeavor; and be glad, that, for one, Clarice was there to hear him,--she heard him so gladly.
12374-8{_}1470{_}To take a vow for Gabriel, to give him to God, to confirm him in possession of the name she had bestowed, became the desire of Clarice.
12374-8{_}1471{_}One day when she had some business to transact in the market, she dressed Gabriel in a new frock she had made for him, and took him with her to the Port, carrying him in her arms half the way.
12374-8{_}1472{_}She did not find the minister, but she had tested the sincerity of her desire.
12374-8{_}1473{_}When he came down again to the Bay, as he did the next Sunday, she was waiting to give him the first fruits of his labors there.
12374-8{_}1474{_}He arrived early in the morning, that he might forestall the fishermen and their families in whatever arrangements they might be making for the day.
12374-8{_}1475{_}When Clarice first saw him, her heart for a moment failed her,--she wished he had not come, or that she had gone off to spend the day before she knew of his coming.
12374-8{_}1476{_}But, in the very midst of her regrets, she caught up Gabriel and walked forth to meet the preacher.
12374-8{_}1477{_}The missionary recognized Clarice, and he had already heard the story of the child.
12374-8{_}1478{_}He was the first to speak, and a few moments' talk, which seemed to her endless, though it was about Gabriel, passed before she could tell him how she had sought him in his own home on account of the boy, and what her wish was concerning him.
12374-8{_}1479{_}A naturalist, walking along that beach and discovering some long-sought specimen, at a moment when he least looked and hoped for it, would have understood the feeling and the manner of the missionary just then.
12374-8{_}1480{_}Surprise came before gladness, and then followed much investigation, whereby the minister would persuade himself, even as the naturalist under similar circumstances would do, of the genuineness of what was before him;--he must ascertain all the attending circumstances.
12374-8{_}1481{_}It was a simple story that his questioning drew forth.
12374-8{_}1482{_}The missionary learned something in the interview, as well as Clarice.
12374-8{_}1483{_}He learned what confidence there is in a noble spirit of resignation; that it need not be the submission of helplessness.
12374-8{_}1484{_}He saw anew, what he had learned for himself under different circumstances, the satisfaction arising from industry that is based on duty, and involves skill in craft, judgment in affairs, and that integrity which keeps one to his oath, though it be not to his profit.
12374-8{_}1485{_}He heard the voice of a tender, pitiful, loving womanhood, strongly manifesting its right to protect helplessness, by the utterance of its convictions concerning that helplessness.
12374-8{_}1486{_}He knew that to such a woman the Master would have spoken not one word of reproach, but many of encouragement and sympathy.
12374-8{_}1487{_}So he spoke to her of courage, and shared her hopes, by directing them with a generous confidence in her.
12374-8{_}1488{_}He was the man for his vocation, for in every strait he looked to his human heart for direction,--and in his heart were not only sympathy and gentleness, but justice and judgment.
12374-8{_}1489{_}While he talked to Clarice, the idea which had taken cognizance of Gabriel alone enlarged,--it involved herself.
12374-8{_}1490{_}"What doth hinder me to be baptized?"
12374-8{_}1491{_}she asked, in the words of Philip.
12374-8{_}1492{_}"If thou believest, thou mayest."
12374-8{_}1493{_}Accordingly, at the conclusion of the morning prayer, when the preacher said, "Those persons to be baptized may now come forward," Clarice Briton, leading little Gabriel by the hand, rose from her seat and walked up before the congregation, and stood in the presence of all.
12374-8{_}1494{_}Not an eye was turned from her during the ceremony.
12374-8{_}1495{_}When she lifted Gabriel, and held him in her arms, and promised the solemn promises for him as well as for herself, the souls that witnessed it thought that they had lost Clarice.
12374-8{_}1496{_}The tears rolled down Old Briton's cheeks when he looked upon the girl.
12374-8{_}1497{_}What he saw he did not half understand, but there was an awful solemnity about the transaction, that overpowered him.
12374-8{_}1498{_}He and Dame Briton had come to the meeting because Clarice urged them to do so;--she had said she was going to make a public promise about Gabriel, and that was all she told them; for, beside that there was little time for explanation in the hurry of preparing Gabriel and herself, Clarice's heart was too deeply stirred to admit of speech.
12374-8{_}1499{_}After she had obtained the promise of her parents, she said no more to them; they did not hear her speak again until her firm "I will" broke on their ears.
12374-8{_}1500{_}Dame Briton was not half pleased at what she saw and heard, during this service.
12374-8{_}1501{_}She looked at Bondo Emmins to see what he was thinking,--but little she learned from his solemn face.
